WILL, PANOS PAUL MORPHOS and RICHARD L. FRAUJTSCHI For Germanic Languages: JOHN G. KUNSTMANN For Italian: JosEPH G. FUCILLA For Spanish and Portuguese: KARL-LUDWIG SELIG and JACKI H. PARKER HARDIN CRAIG, Advisory Editor. ANNOUNCEMENT To insure the noting of a'l recent Renaissance studies in the bibliography to the published in the April, 1961, issue of Studies in Philology, the editors request all authors of articles on Renaissance topics published during 1960 to send copies of their offprints as soon as they appear to Editor, " Renaissance Bibliography" Studies in Philology, Box 149 University of North Carolina Chapel Hill, N. C. 221</page><page sequence="2">This bibliography includes important Renaissance items pub- lished and available to American scholars during the year 1959. As a rule, reprints, translations, and new editions are noticed only when they embody new material or have other distinctive features. Works entered with one or more reviews in previous numbers of the bibliography are not reentered unless more than one additional review has come to the editors' attention. They will be included in subsequent issues when the number of reviews warrants notice. When the year of publication is not given, 1959 is to be under- stood. Reference is made to earlier numbers of the bibliography by year of publication. Thus, reference to an entry in " Literature of the Renaissance in 1958," published in the April number of Studies in Philology for 1959, will be indicated in "See 1959: " followed by the entry number. Various indebtednesses have been incurred by the editors. For all such assistance they extend their thanks. They are especially grateful to the staffs of the libraries in which they have done their work. Thanks are due Claudia L. C. Wogan for her assistance in compiling the Index. 222</page><page sequence="3">CONTENTS PAGE GENERAL WORKS OF TIE RENAISSANCE .227 ENGLISH I. General Works: Bibliographical and Critical. Language. The First Elegy " is not a direct comparison of Milton with Ovid, but a cross-comparison: Milton's exile to London is as happy as Ovid's residence in Rome; Milton's residence at Cambridge is as miserable as Ovid's exile to Tomis." The sections on the theatre and on English women awkwardly lie outside the plan given by the cross-comparison. E785. Daniels, Edgar F. "The God of Paradise Lost and the Divina Commedia." Archiv f. Stud., 195 Bd, 273-89. The two poets' approaches to the concept of God " were not ... different solutions to the same poetic or theological problem, the same aesthetic dilemma. They were . . . specific responses to different literary requirements, and they reflected a deliberate emphasis on different aspects of deity." Dante finds the supreme beatitude in visio Dei; Milton stresses the concept of Providence. E831. Steadman, John M. " Heroic Virtue and the Divine Image in Paradise Lost." Jour.
